# 三洞珠囊

**The Three Caverns' Pearl Bag**

**Title:** *The Three Caverns' Pearl Bag*
Compiled by Wang Tihe of the Tang. Ten scrolls.
Source text: *Daoist Canon of the Orthodox Unity*, *Taiping* Section.

**Scroll One of *The Three Caverns' Pearl Bag*: The Chapter on Salvation and Guidance**

The *Biographies of Daoist Scholars* (Chapter Four) states: Ren Dun, styled Shangneng, was a native of Bochang. During the Yongjia era, he retreated to Yunyang Mountain—Yunyang being another name for Maoshan. He ingested red halloysite, occasionally venturing among the people while always holding scriptures and precepts in hand, instructing the ignorant masses. Thus, those near and far reverently followed his teachings. Dun sighed in secret: "Though the people claim to admire goodness, it is but an outward show; I have yet to see a single true heart with whom I might cut metal asunder."

It further states: Du Jiong, styled Zigong, in his prime awakened to faith with diligent devotion. He revered the Orthodox Unity tradition, briefly studying talismanic registers under the Celestial Master, using them to transform and guide others, universally aiding and rescuing all. His conduct was pure and meticulous, his heart empty of self as he uplifted others, seeking no alms or offerings. He established a tranquil oratory, widely propagating salvation and protection, with all his works bearing immediate efficacy.

It further states: When Lu Na served as Minister of Works at the age of forty, he suffered from sores and told Jiong, "Generations have been short-lived, and all face this affliction at life’s end." Jiong submitted a memorial to the heavens and gave him the *Divine Flying Powder*. He said, "Your destined calamity has passed; you may live to seventy." And so it came to pass.

**Scroll Five** states: Yan Junping, whose given name was Zun, was a native of Shu’s capital. He cultivated the Dao for self-preservation. When speaking to others’ sons, he grounded his words in filial piety; to ministers, in loyalty; to younger brothers, in obedience. Adapting to each person’s inclinations, he guided them toward goodness. The people of Shu were transformed by him, and more than half followed his counsel.

It further states: Liu Ningzhi, styled Zhi’an and nicknamed Longevity, was a native of Zhijiang in Nanjun. Dwelling on the southern slopes of Mount Heng, he gathered herbs and practiced ingestion, receiving the Celestial Master’s teachings on transforming the people. He and his wife both wore talismans, rescuing others from disasters and perils, with frequent divine efficacy.

**Scroll Six** states: Zhu Huikai, styled Zhiyuan, was a native of Wucheng in Wuxing. He devoted himself to precepts, self-cultivation, and aiding others. In the eighth year of the Daming era of the Qi, when famine struck the land, Huikai had a modest store of grain. He distributed it all to relieve the destitute, and his village depended on him. Three starving men ate their fill and died, their families suing the county, accusing Huikai of feeding them to death and slandering him bitterly. The magistrate laughed and said, "To die from eating one’s fill after begging—should one resent the host? There is no law for this." He dismissed the case without inquiry.

**Scroll Seven** states: Master Lu was summoned by Emperor Wen of the Song to the palace, where he expounded on principles and the Dao without pause, day or night, tirelessly instructing and exhorting.

**Scroll Eight** states: Tao Hongjing, styled Tongming, was a native of Pingyang in Weijun. He styled himself the Recluse of Huayang, delighting in secret virtue, aiding the distressed and impoverished, and constantly preparing efficacious remedies. He dispensed medicines to the sick, and those near and far relied on him.

**Scroll Nine** states: Pan Hong, styled Wensheng, was a native of Shanyin in Kuaiji. By nature, he loved to bestow alms. Each winter, he repeatedly made winter garments to distribute to the poor, moving from house to house to provide them.

**Scroll Ten** states: Dongxiang Zongchao, styled Yilun, was a native of Qianzou in Gaomi. During years of famine, he frequently provided large quantities of food, drawing crowds from near and far at midday. Each winter, he made many lined jackets, distributing them as circumstances allowed.

It further states: Yan Jizhi, styled Jingchu, was a native of Jurong in Danyang. He was indifferent to wealth and fond of giving, aiding the distressed and impoverished. Well-versed in the Dao and its principles, whenever a county official fell ill, he secretly performed rituals, privately arranging matters and offering thanks at the proper time.

It further states: Fang Qianzhi, styled Daochong, was a native of Baixian in Zhaojun, Jizhou. He entered the secluded Tianmu Mountain, transforming the villages and countryside, turning evil to good, urging people to seek learning and embrace benevolence, and turning toward the Great Dao.

It further states: Burying the exposed bones of the dead and interring the dry remains of the forgotten, he aided the destitute in times of urgency. Though Prince Shaoling repeatedly sent invitations, Qianzhi refused to meet him. When the prince’s envoys arrived at his residence, he would occasionally encounter them and generously bestow gifts.

It further states: Zhang Xuanche, styled Wenju, was a native of Yiyang Commandery in Sizhou. At the end of the Liang, when great chaos reigned, he followed others eastward to eke out a living and preserve his life. Yet he always shared a pot with the starving, cooking together. Xuanche was kind and yielding, never putting himself first. He constantly pushed the cauldron toward others, letting them take precedence. Even when there was but a handful of cooked food, he always ate last, doing so day after day. Gradually, he grew weak and emaciated, and one day, he passed away. Because he had always put others before himself, he was reborn in a better existence.

It further states: Chu Ya, styled Xuantong, was a native of Qiantang in Wu Commandery. At the end of the Liang, he lived in reclusion on Mount Juqu. He valued giving and disdained wealth, tirelessly aiding others. When his fields yielded a harvest, he gave the grain to the poor. Living among others, he always rose early to sweep and fetch water, ensuring all were provided for, even gathering firewood for beggars. In summer, he planted melons and allowed people to take them freely.

It further states: Zhang Min, styled Gaoming, was a native of Xuancheng. He planted pines and oaks, and whatever was left or given to him, he kept nothing, especially delighting in bestowing alms. He accumulated no wealth, dispersing whatever he received as soon as he obtained it. Whenever he encountered perilous situations or places where prohibited items were found, or when fellow travelers faced public or private obstacles they could not overcome, Min would find a way to carry them through, ensuring their safe passage. On the night of ascending the altar, when the cold was bitter and severe, Min bared his left hand to hold the candle, enduring until dawn without slackening. Whenever he attended others’ rituals, he personally performed menial tasks, sweeping away filth and exerting all his strength.

In the heat, he would yield his curtains and canopy; in the cold, he would offer his clothing and bedding.

Volume Thirteen states: Meng Daoyang, styled Xiaoyuan and also known as Yuan externally, was a native of Pingchang. His nature was kind and benevolent, and he devoted himself entirely to almsgiving. During years of famine, many starved to death, so he frequently prepared food to distribute. He often lamented his poverty, for it prevented him from giving as generously as he wished.

Volume Fourteen states: Sun Youyue, styled Xuanda, was a native of Yongkang in Dongyang. He diligently guided and received disciples, and those who reverently studied under him numbered in the hundreds.

It further states: The Twin Masters of Discourse were men of the Liang dynasty. They abstained from grains, observed prolonged fasts, and practiced asceticism. They traveled to Guangzhou to propagate the teachings, and the people of Nanhai regarded them as their masters.

It further states: Xu Mingye, a native of Chigang in Fufeng, lived during the Taiqing era of the Liang dynasty. He left home in his youth, observed a strict vegetarian diet, and wandered through mountains and rivers, dedicating himself to relief and salvation. During a time of famine when the people suffered hardship, Mingye constantly provided aid. He ensured that all who came received food and drink. Leading his disciples, he tilled the fields and sowed seeds. When the rice and millet were nearly ripe, he traveled near and far, summoning the poor to harvest according to their strength. If he encountered someone suffering from cold, he would remove his own clothes to give them. In the depths of winter, he wore only a single layer of cloth. Whenever he entered the city to perform meritorious deeds, whatever he received he immediately distributed to the needy, never carrying anything out through the city gates. If someone was ill, he would forgo all other meritorious deeds, employing every expedient means to attend to them, for he believed that without his care, their lives would not be preserved. If he visited a household and suddenly departed in haste, it was certain that misfortune or disaster would befall that place.

Volume Fifteen states: Lu Yichong, styled Jingyou, was a native of Yangdu in Haiguan. From a young age, he was deeply devoted to the Daoist teachings. In his interactions with others, he treated the noble and the humble with equal regard. Skilled in comforting the masses, he became a refuge for those near and far.

It further states: Zou Rong, styled Wenhui, was a native of Zhang’an in Linhai. He left home in his youth and was diligent in his duties. He treated all without partiality.

Volume Sixteen states: Yin Zhongkan, a native of Chenjun, served as Senior Attendant to the Crown Prince. From his youth, he revered the Heavenly Master Dao, receiving both the *zhihi* and *Zhengyi* ordinations. He served the teachings with devotion, sparing no wealth. When illness struck his household, he personally inscribed talismans, and they often proved efficacious. When villagers or attendants sought his aid, he would promptly provide relief, bringing no small benefit.

Volume Eighteen states: Deng Yuzhi, styled Xuanda, was a native of Xinye in Nanyang. He once dreamed of a crow that spat out a seal and gave it to him. Thereafter, whenever the common folk fell ill, he would treat and heal them with the seal alone, without inscribing talismans, and the afflicted would recover of their own accord.

It further states: Zhuge Lin, styled Maolun, was a native of Langye. Devoted to the Dao, he maintained purity and often fasted to aid others in illness. When treating others or himself, he took no medicine or herbs, drinking only consecrated water, and none failed to recover. During the *Taiyuan* era, Lin’s eyelids suddenly developed growths. He sought water from the Daoist sanctuary to wash them, and after a few days, they gradually healed.

It further states: Puyang—of unknown origin—served the Dao with single-minded devotion, and his prayers were always efficacious. A girl surnamed Zheng, whose given name sounds like *ti* and who suffered from a lame foot, came to him to wash her foot with the consecrated water. The remainder was poured onto a withered jujube tree in the courtyard, and the tree revived while her foot simultaneously healed. After Emperor Jianwen of Jin deposed the heir apparent and later had no sons, Puyang was then in the palace and secretly offered prayers. At the third watch, a yellow *qi* arose from the southwest and descended directly into the chamber. That night, Empress Dowager Li conceived Emperor Xiaowu—such was the power of the unseen Dao.

Volume Twenty states: Ji Huiyan, a native of Yuhang in Wuxing, left home in her youth to become a *bhikṣuṇī*, later renouncing her vows to become a Daoist nun. She then resided on Mount Tianmu, practicing grain avoidance and consuming medicinal foods. Whenever someone fell gravely ill, she would administer a talisman, and all were instantly cured.

*The Upper Register of Immortals* records: At the foot of Mount Tai, an old man named Shifu sold medicines in the marketplace. He claimed to be three hundred years old. When a pestilence later struck and tens of thousands perished, the prefect visited Shifu to seek aid. Shifu carried a vermilion banner and distributed yellow powder through the stricken quarters; all who drank it recovered, numbering in the tens of thousands.

*The Lower Register of Immortals* records: Chang Rong, a Daoist of Mount Chang, called herself a daughter of the Yin king. She ate the roots of *penglei* (creeping fig), and though she was seen by many over two hundred years, her complexion remained that of a woman of twenty. She gathered purple grass, sold it to dyers, and gave the money to orphans and widows. This she did for generations, and ten thousand households came to venerate her.

*The Third Scroll of the Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: Shen Xi, a man of Wu Commandery, studied the Dao in Shu. He could only dispel misfortune, cure illnesses, and aid the common people.

The Fourth Scroll records: Huang Luzi, surnamed Ge and named Yue, treated illness by having patients from a thousand *li* away send their names to him; all were cured without his ever seeing them in person.

Volume Five records: Shen Jian, a native of Danyang, was skilled in healing. No matter how severe the illness, all who met him recovered.

The Eighth Scroll records: Wang Yao, styled Boliao, was a man of Poyang. He practiced healing with unfailing success, yet performed no sacrifices, used no talismanic water, needles, or medicines. When treating illness, he would simply spread an eight-foot cloth on the ground and sit upon it, neither eating nor drinking. In a moment, the patient would recover, and he would depart. If malevolent spirits caused harm, Yao would draw a prison on the ground, summon them aloud, and their forms—foxes, raccoon dogs, or serpents—would appear within. He would then slay and burn them, and the afflicted would immediately recover.

The Ninth Scroll records: Li Changzai, a man of Shu Commandery, tended to the critically ill and cured them in three days; those less afflicted recovered in a single day. If a case was incurable, he would not attend to it.

It further states: There was a man named Gan Jun, a native of Beihai. He had suffered from leprosy for decades, and no medicine could cure him. One day, he saw an old medicine vendor in the market named Bo He and went to inquire of him. The old man said, "Your illness can be treated. If you truly wish to recover, come to the northern side of the great bridge beneath the magnolia tree at the crowing of the rooster tomorrow, and I shall teach you." The next day, Gan Jun arrived at the appointed place at cockcrow, only to find Bo He already there. The old man grew angry and said, "Do you not wish to be cured? Why have you come late?" He then set a new time for the middle of the night the following day. Thus, Gan Jun arrived at the appointed place at sunset and waited. After a moment, the old man appeared. Gan Jun asked, "Is this not the proper time?" The old man then handed him two volumes of plain script, admonishing him, "With these books, you will not only recover from your illness but attain longevity." Gan Jun bowed twice and received the books. The old man further instructed, "Return home and copy these books until you have one hundred and fifty volumes." Gan Jun pondered their meaning and, applying their teachings inwardly to cultivate his body and nurture his nature, and outwardly to dispel calamities and heal illnesses, found that none failed to be cured. He lived among the people for over three hundred years before achieving the Dao and ascending as an immortal.

*The Hidden Instructions on Ascending to Perfection* records: The True Man of the Southern Peak and Left Immortal Duke, Supreme Perfected Lord of Grand Void, surnamed Chi and named Zhongying, initially studied the Dao on Mount Jinhua. Suddenly, he fell gravely ill and remained so for sixteen years. The Azure Lad bestowed upon him the *Scripture of Wisdom for Dispelling Demons*, and by reciting it three thousand times while aiding his condition, he fully recovered.

*The Seventh Scroll of Daoist Biographies* states: Lu Xiujing, styled Yuande, was a native of Dongqian in Wuxing. In the seventh year of the Dashizu era of the Song, when Emperor Ming fell ill, Master Lu led the assembly in performing the Three Primes Open-Air Fast for the state’s prayers. On the twentieth day, clouds gathered, winds rose sharply, and a light rain dampened the dust. At the second watch, a yellow qi resembling a precious canopy suddenly ascended from below before the hall, rising some ten zhang in height. It spread over the steps and courtyard for several moments, transforming into five colors that illuminated the eaves and beams. After lingering for a long while, it suddenly revolved and ascended to the scripture platform, where it dispersed and vanished. Over a hundred people who witnessed the fast all saw this, and the event was reported to the emperor, whose illness improved. It was deemed an auspicious sign.

It further states: Meng Jingyi, styled Fuming, was a native of Anqiu in Pingchang. When Prince Jingling of Liang obtained a set of *Numinous Treasure Scriptures*, he casually tossed them aside. Soon after, his hands broke out in carbuncles, causing excruciating pain. He then sent Master Feng to offer apologies. The master composed a repentance text, expressing deep remorse, and though Meng’s condition slightly improved, he ultimately died from this affliction.

The Eighteenth Scroll records: Lou Anle was a native of Qiaoguo. His wife, née Fu, had suffered from wind sores for over a decade, and despite countless treatments, none proved effective. She devoted herself solely to the Daoist gate, hoping for deliverance. In the seventh year of the Yuanjia era of the Song, during a violent storm, the roof of Lou Anle’s elder brother’s house collapsed. Fearing her own home would also cave in and crush her, Fu rushed into the central courtyard, forgetting her foot ailment. Thereupon, her condition returned to normal, a result of her faith in the Dao. How could medicine alone be relied upon?

*The Scripture of the Mysterious Mother’s Eight Gates* states: The immortal Zhao Chengzi consumed five minerals, which erupted from his body, and he died on Mount Xuan in Shanggu, Youzhou. Six years later, a traveler passing through the mountains saw his rotting corpse with the stones exposed in his abdomen. The traveler took and swallowed them, then departed. Four or five years later, the stones flew out and returned to the mouth of the former corpse, which then revived, its face radiating jade-like light. The Five Elder Immortals gnashed their teeth in rebuke at this thief of the five viscera’s treasures. This leper-faced man—his face afflicted with leprosy—would, upon reaching the first gate, suffer leprosy of varying severity, leading to the death of his entire clan.

*The First Scripture of Divine Incantations* states: Blue qi portends sudden death; red qi, swelling and illness; yellow qi, dysentery; white qi, cholera; black qi, official misfortune. The Red-Headed Ghost wields these qi to spread across the world, slaying the wicked.

*The Hidden Instructions on Ascending to Perfection* records: Cui Wenzhi—*The Biographies of the Immortals* states he was a native of Taishan—healed the sick with medicines at the foot of Mount Tai. Chi Jun, who had been ill on Mount Jinhua for sixteen years, later recovered from his wind affliction and attained the Dao.

*The Fifth Declaration of Perfected Realities* states: Wind afflictions arise from the dampness and stagnation of the three springs in the yin of hills and graves. Thus, the Earth Officials, through the clashing of water and qi, often produce wind impediment. In severe cases, the entire body becomes unresponsive; in milder cases, half the body or the loss of hands and feet occurs. If one frequently dreams of the northeast or northwest, passing through former dwellings or seeing spirit groves, it is precisely because one is encountering the qi of tombs. The northeast of a grave slightly severs life, while the northwest is the Nine Perils, both inauspicious grounds for tomb disputes. If one sees the deceased there, it is surely a sign. For those who frequently have such dreams, upon waking, face upward, tap the teeth three times, and recite the following incantation:

*"Supreme Primordial, Purple Heaven of the Nine Capitals,
Regulate the hun-soul, protect life,
Lofty and Pure True Person,
I receive the supreme method, the teachings of Grand Mystery,
To attain long life and enduring vision,
Body soaring, form immortal,
Tombs eternally at peace,
Ghostly disputes and treachery sealed,
Hun and po harmonious and joyful,
Evil qi dispelled,
Specters and shadowy beings,
Dare not offend my spirit.
The Northern Emperor strikes and subdues,
Gathering the qi into the abyss,
Recorded in the registers of the Supreme August,
Respectfully reported to the Jade Dawn."*

Recite this twice, then tap the teeth three more times, and one will no longer dream of tombs or deceased family ghosts. For those who frequently have inauspicious dreams, this method may also be applied. Thus, ghostly qi will vanish, and malevolent spirits will scatter.

Moreover, it is said: When the arms fail to respond, it is because stagnant wind-toxin qi has settled in the meridians, congealing upon the bones and causing numbness. Acupuncture and moxibustion should be applied, for these will bring cure. It is also fitting to invoke the Northern Emperor with the "Prayer of Bending and Turning." If performed a hundred times, the affliction will likewise dissipate.

First, slowly massage the afflicted arm with one hand for a long while, then close the eyes and inwardly visualize. Swallow saliva three times, tap the teeth three rounds, compose the mind, and softly intone:

"Supreme Four Mysteries, Five Flowers and Six Courts,
Three *hun*-souls and seven *po*-souls,
Celestial Pass and Earthly Essence,
Divine Palace and Protective Guardians,
Heavenly Womb and Lofty Clarity,
The hundred spirits of the four limbs,
The myriad numina of the nine joints—
Receive the registers of Jade Dawn,
Inscribed in the Jade City.
Jade maidens attend my body,
Jade lads guard my life.
Ever aligned with the Two Radiant Ones,
I ascend as an immortal to the Upper Clarity,
My years enduring with sun and moon.
Later, I shall soar beyond, attaining the Great Peace.
Yet now, flowing wind and congealed infirmity,
Possessing ghosts and five flying [demons],
*Wangliang* and tomb-qi,
Yin-qi lingering and encroaching,
Assail my four limbs,
Offend my flourishing and decline.
Supreme Celestial Ding,
Dragon and Tiger display their might,
Slaying inauspicious ghosts,
Crushing wind and malevolence.
Examine and restrain hidden disputes,
Concealed poisons and hidden wrongs,
Restore me to my former state,
My radiance equal to sun and moon.
Should any dare to test me,
The Northern Star shall seize and shatter them,
Defying the luminous majesty above."

In ancient times, Tang Lan dwelled in the forests of Linlü Mountain when he was struck by a ghost, leaving his entire body unresponsive, as if wrapped in silk floss. A Daoist taught him this method, and his affliction was immediately dispelled. This is the "Bending and Turning" method of the Northern Emperor. For all ailments involving bending and turning, this method is excellent—not merely for wind-numbness and unresponsiveness.

Tang Lan now resides on Mount Hua, having obtained the *Hongdan* method and achieved immortality through its practice. Zheng Zizhen was the grandson of Kangcheng. Now dwelling on Mount Yangdi, he once suffered for years from unresponsive legs. In his later years, he combined acupuncture and moxibustion with the "Bending and Turning" prayer, and within a hundred days, his ailment was entirely cured.

The onset of wind and *kao*-qi occurs through gaps in weakened qi. When the body is deficient, illness invades. If one recovers and truly reduces frivolous exertions, wind-infirmities will surely be dispelled. Now, to restrain and control the qi of tomb-infestations, one may also present the "Tomb Dispute Petition," which I shall submit on your behalf. Thus, the infesting qi will be severed.

Formerly, Deng Yunshan, having attained the Dao, suddenly found both hands unresponsive. I sent word instructing him to apply moxibustion to *Fenghui* and *Qujin*. Within six or seven days, he could perform the "Five Animals Exercise." After the acupuncture is complete, one should focus on the moxibustion sites and inwardly circulate the Dao, which is no different from physical practice.

In the past, Lord Zhao Cheng’s legs dragged, leaving him unable to rise. Morning and night, he constantly visualized bowing to the Supreme. After thirty years, the Supreme True Person bestowed upon him a dose of *Liu Ming Tan Huan* powder. Immediately, he could stand and walk, and later attained the Dao. He now resides beneath Mount Heming.

It is further said: Upon waking at night, visualize the solar orb grasped in the afflicted hand. Let the sun’s crimson rays reverse from the arm to the elbow and armpit. After a long while, the rays transform into fire, burning the arm until it is thoroughly permeated inside and out. When this is complete, silently intone:

"Fourfold Clarity of the Upper Origin,
Qi of sun and moon divided,
Flowing light resplendent,
Irrigating the soul with congealed essence.
Divine fire scatters radiance,
Purging filth and refining vapors,
Permeating wind-qi,
Burning away the hundred evils.
Grant me long life,
My four limbs whole.
Infesting *kao*-ghosts and harming qi,
Consigned to the Northern Star."

For students of the Dao, one must first treat illness, ensuring the body is free of vacuous evil, blood deficiency, brain depletion, or stagnant and turbid fluids. Without first treating illness, even the ingestion of medicinals and circulation of qi will bring no benefit to the body.

Formerly, the Daoist Wang Zhongfu, from his youth, was devoted to the affairs of divine immortals. For over forty years, he practiced the "Absorbing the Two Radiant Ones and Feeding on Clouds" method, yet perceived no benefit. His son also practiced it for eighteen years before ascending to heaven in broad daylight. Later, the True Person of Southern Marchmount descended and instructed Zhongfu: "The reason you have not attained transcendence is that your body suffers from grave illness—your brain palace is depleted, sinew fluids unmoistened, and numinous essences unsurging. Though you connect with true radiance and feed on clouds, it has not benefited your body."

Zhongfu then treated his illness with medicinals while continuing his practice. After another eighteen years, he too ascended to heaven in broad daylight. Now on the Dark Continent, he received scriptures and became the True Person of the Central Marchmount, presiding over the Nine Mysteries. He remains there to this day.

*The Oral Instructions of the Phoenix Net* states: When a Daoist falls ill, close the eyes and inwardly visualize the heart generating fire to burn the body. Maintain this visualization until the essence is as if tangible, and illness will be cured. This is the method of "storing fire in pain," most secret and efficacious.

*The Declarations of the Perfected, Scroll Seven* records: Li Zheng, before entering the mountains, also suffered from wind-numbness, which persisted for a long time before he recovered.

It further states: Fan Bocai of Guiyang was originally a layman, but suddenly fell victim to madness, demonic possession, and consumptive illness. He lay bedridden for years, and though his family summoned masters to resolve the matter, their expenses depleted his wealth, yet his illness remained uncured. Hearing that the Great Dao was pure and simple, requiring nothing, his resolve changed. Learning that Shen Jing had become a diligent Daoist with proven success in treating illness, he abandoned his family and worldly affairs. After fifty days, his illness was entirely cured. Later, he entered Mount Tianmu, consumed sesame, and became the True Person of Primordial Unity.

*The Declarations of the Perfected, Scroll Eight* records: Formerly, Bao Zhu of Jibei suddenly suffered from whirling wind. His mouth and eyes became crooked, wind-qi entered his mouth, and his upper and lower teeth incessantly gnashed with loud clattering, day and night without cease. He lived to the age of one hundred twenty-seven.

*The Declarations of the Perfected, Scroll Nine* records: Yang Xi’s third daughter recently suffered from consumptive decline; though she has lately improved somewhat, she still has not ventured outside.

It further states: Xu Yufu remarked that Academician Yang’s younger brother was gravely ill and utterly weakened.

*The Declarations of the Perfected, Scroll Ten* records: Xu Que, styled Xiyuan, was the Marquis of Jindu Township. Later, he suffered from wind-illness and lost the power of speech. He died in the second year of the Long’an era at the age of seventy.

*The Transmission of Daoist Learning, Scroll Four* records: During a great epidemic, the people rushed to Wu Meng to beg for water. Meng, troubled by the incessant demands, drew water from the river a hundred paces away, allowing them to take it as they wished. All who received the water were cured.

It further states: The Daoist Shu Daoyun had suffered from malaria for three years, and no treatment had brought relief. Wu Meng taught him the *Three Sovereigns’ Poems* and instructed him to recite them aloud. As soon as he began, his illness was instantly cured.

It further records: Dai Ganlu suffered from a loathsome disease and was exiled to the coastal regions. Yun Mingzheng declared, “This can be cured.” After several days of treatment, he was fully restored.

It further states: The Prefectural Administrator Kang Hou of great wealth had a daughter who had been ill for years. Despite consulting countless physicians and invoking every Daoist method, her condition only worsened. Yun Mingzheng arrived at his gate and declared, “I can cure your daughter.” Kang Hou, filled with arrogance, refused to receive him. Having encountered many who claimed mastery of arts yet failed to deliver results, and seeing Mingzheng in tattered clothes and barefoot, he dismissed him as a madman and did not believe him. Mingzheng insisted he could cure long-standing ailments and requested to see the daughter. The Prefect grew furious, for his daughter’s illness had persisted so long that no one could have restored her. Suddenly, the daughter asked her household, “Who wishes to see me?” She then dressed herself, put on her shoes, and emerged, adorned and composed. The Prefect was astonished. Mingzheng said, “The young lady is already cured. She may return inside.” Only then did the entire household begin to acknowledge his divine efficacy.

Scroll Four further records: Ren Dun, styled Shang, possessed the ability to heal illnesses. Those afflicted with the most severe and incurable diseases, abandoned by all hope of recovery, received his treatment and were invariably saved. Such cases were numerous. He spoke little, though when he did, he would foretell future fortunes or misfortunes, and all came to pass as he said. When asked about an illness, if he replied, “There is no suffering; it is not life-threatening,” the patient would surely recover. If he remained silent, none could be saved.

It further states: Du Jiong, styled Zigong, was skilled in healing illnesses and could foresee the good and evil among people. Long Zhi of Shangyu and Si Shen of Qiantang, both shamans, envied Jiong’s mastery of the Dao and constantly slandered him. When informed of this, Jiong said, “To slander the true Dharma is to soon invite karmic retribution.” Shortly after, Zhi’s wife died suddenly, and Shen was stricken with a hidden illness. Both repented and sought forgiveness. Jiong performed rites to absolve them, and they were instantly cured. Later, Shen fell ill again. Jiong said, “You harbor ghostly objects, which is why the qi haunts you.” Shen confessed, “I indeed hid a chest of fine clothes.” Jiong retrieved and burned them, and Shen’s illness vanished at once.

It further records: Wang Xizhi fell ill and summoned Du Jiong. Jiong said to his disciples, “Wang Youjun’s illness will not be cured. What need has he for me?” More than ten days later, Wang passed away.

It further states: When Lu Na served as Minister of Works at the age of forty, he suffered from sores. He told Jiong, “My family has been short-lived for generations, and all have died afflicted by these sores.” Jiong submitted a memorial to the heavens on his behalf and gave him the *Divine Flying Powder*. He said, “Your destined hardship has passed. You will live to seventy.” And so it came to be.

Scroll Five records: An Qiu [missing character], styled Zhongdu, was a native of Changling in Jingzhao during the reign of Emperor Cheng. Gravely ill, his disciples placed him beneath a tree in the courtyard. An Qiu suddenly awoke, fully recovered. It was winter, yet he smelled the fragrance of plums. Opening his eyes, he saw two red plums hanging from a withered branch. His disciple Du reached up to catch them, and the plums fell into his palm. An Qiu ate them, and all his suffering vanished. His body felt light, his vision clear. He then departed with Du, and none knew where they went.

It further states: Guo Wen, styled Wenju, was a native of Henan. Stricken by a severe epidemic, he endured great suffering but refused medicine, saying, “My fate does not depend on medicine.” He fasted for over twenty days yet did not grow emaciated. Later, he passed away and was buried in Lin’an County of Yuhang.

It further records: Liu Ningzhi, styled Zhi’an, with the childhood name Changnian, was a native of Zhijiang in Nanjun. From a young age, he suffered from frailty, dizziness, and confusion, and his condition worsened over the years.

Scroll Seven records: Lu Xiujing, styled Yuande, was a native of Dongqian in Wuxing during the Song dynasty. He secluded himself in Yunmeng Mountain to cultivate the Dao. While briefly descending to seek medicine, he passed through his hometown and stayed with his family for several days. Suddenly, his daughter fell gravely ill, her life hanging by a thread. His family urgently begged him to save her. The Master sighed and said, “I have long severed ties with wife and children, entrusting my body to the profound Dao. My return home is like staying at an inn—how could I still harbor attachment?” With that, he brushed his sleeves and departed without a backward glance. The day after he left, his daughter’s illness was cured.

It further states: Xiujing had long suffered from a qi ailment. Carrying medicine, he entered the mountains and stayed in a separate chamber. Soon after, it was engulfed in flames. His disciples rushed to extinguish the fire, but the Master said, “There is no need to save it. This is the will of the unseen realm, forbidding me to keep the medicine. My illness will soon be cured on its own.” Within days, he recovered.

Scroll Eight records: Gu Huan, styled Xuanping, also styled Jingyi, was a native of Wu in Wu Commandery. The village of Baishan was plagued by malevolent illnesses. The villagers pleaded for his help. Huan went to the village and lectured on the *Laozi*, drawing the hells upon the ground. After a while, foxes, badgers, turtles, and alligators entered the depicted hells in great numbers, and the afflicted were all cured.

Scroll Ten records: Fang Qianzhi, styled Daochong, was a native of Baixian in Zhaojun, Jizhou. From a young age, he abstained from alcohol and never held a cup in his hands until his old age. Though he fell ill, he never took medicine or underwent acupuncture, entrusting his life and death to fate while aiding others.

It further states: Zhang Xuanche, styled Wenju, was a native of Yiyang Commandery. While discussing matters with his fellow villager Zhang Guisun, Guisun suddenly suffered a wind-induced illness and could not rise or move. Xuanche abandoned his studies to personally care for him, handling filthy vessels, overseeing meals, and tending to his needs for three years without complaint. The people of his time admired his loyalty and resolve.

Scroll Twelve records: Jia Leng, styled Xuanmiao, was a native of Chengdu in Shu Commandery. In his youth, he became a Buddhist monk but returned to secular life after encountering bandits. Later in life, he embraced the Dao and debated right and wrong with various Daoists. In his old age, he fell ill, his lips and teeth failing to close, his speech impeded, hindering his access to the wondrous gate. The Dharma Seat sighed, “This illness of mine must have been brought on by offending the Dao. Much of what I have written has been lost.”

Scroll Fifteen records: Huan Kai, styled Yanshu, was a native of Dantu in Donghai. At the beginning of the Liang dynasty, three ancient lacquered bamboo containers were found in the Kunlun Mountains, amid the level sands of an islet. Inside were three copies of the *Scripture of Great Peace*, written on yellow silk and transmitted by Lord Gan.

The villagers marveled, and widely established quiet offerings at the scripture’s place of origin. Huan Kai then requested a copy from the villagers to take back to the capital for veneration. He first presented it to Lord Tao, who declared, “This is indeed the ancient text of Lord Gan.” Upon hearing that the scripture was to be brought to the capital, he fell gravely ill with malaria, and no treatment could cure him. Tao Hongjing, styled Zhenbai, heard of this and said, “This illness is not mine to fear—it must be the scripture’s retribution. Why not return it to its original place?” Following his advice, the scripture was sent back, and the illness was cured.

Scroll Sixteen records: Kong Lingchan, styled Lingchan, was a native of Shanyin in Kuaiji. When his mother passed away, he observed mourning with such filial devotion that he abstained from wine and delicacies, living on gruel, vegetables, and plain cloth for the rest of his life. His father, unaware of this, was in the capital. Later, when Lingchan went to the capital to pay his respects, his father saw his emaciated state and, moved with pity, ordered the kitchen to prepare fine dishes and shared them with him. Though Lingchan obeyed his father’s kindness and forced himself to eat, he fell ill as a result. His father, recognizing his innate nature could not be changed, did not press him further.

Scroll Nineteen records: Emperor Ming of Qi fell ill and often summoned the monastic assembly to the inner palace to perform Daoist rites. Upon hearing the recitation of scriptures by Yan Zhiming, styled Huishi, a Daoist from Jinling among the assembly, he felt great admiration and delight, saying his illness was eased. After the ritual concluded and Zhiming returned outside, the emperor could not rest at night. He issued an edict summoning Zhiming to recite before him, and upon doing so, he felt joy and relief. The emperor then granted him an imperial carriage and oxen, and whenever he traveled, he was received with honor by both Daoists and laypeople.

Scroll Twenty records: During the reign of Emperor Yuan of Liang, his heir, Fang Deng, fell gravely ill. His consort, Lady Xu, purified her heart and body and sent someone to the female attendant Li Lingcheng at the Hualin Lodge to perform meritorious deeds. That night, the consort dreamed of two young attendants in blue, their appearance and attire unlike ordinary beings, who called themselves the Hualin Attendants. They were sent to inform her that the illness would be cured if a stone from the altar were retrieved and returned. Upon waking, she asked the heir, who said, “Recently, I had a stone taken from the mountain pool for use and sent it back.” He then sent his tutor, Wang Xiaosi, into the mountains to hold a fast and repent, and the heir recovered.

*The Fourth Scroll of the Biographies of Divine Immortals* records: Those afflicted with illness sought healing from Sun Bo of Hedong. Bo did nothing extraordinary—he merely pointed at them, and they were cured.

Scroll Seven records: Dong Feng, styled Junyi, was a native of Houguan. During the reign of the former Lord of Wu, he returned to reside at the foot of Mount Lu in Yuzhang. A man who had suffered from leprosy since youth, on the verge of death, carried himself to Feng and kowtowed, begging for mercy. Feng had the man sit in a room, covering his eyes with five layers of cloth and instructing him not to move. He ordered his household to keep away. The man later recounted hearing something licking him, the pain unbearable, as if rain fell upon every part of him. He estimated the creature’s tongue to be about a foot wide, its breath as large as an ox’s, yet he never saw what it was. After a long while, the creature departed. Feng then removed the blindfold, bathed the man, and sent him away, saying, “You are cured now—just avoid the wind.” Within ten days, the man’s body turned entirely red, his skin gone, and the pain was severe, though bathing in water eased it. After twenty days, new skin grew, and his sores healed, leaving his flesh smooth as congealed fat.

It is also said: Feng lived in the mountains without farming. Though he healed people’s illnesses, he accepted no money or goods. Instead, he asked those cured of serious illnesses to plant five apricot trees for him, and those with minor ailments to plant one. Over several years, the apricot trees numbered in the tens of thousands, forming a lush forest.

Scroll Nine records: Gui Jun was the governor of Xuzhou. He had suffered from leprosy for ten years, and no physician could cure him. Hearing that Lord Gan possessed the Dao, he went to see him. He arrived with hundreds of attendants, their presence imposing. Yet when he reached the gate, Lord Gan did not come out to greet him, and upon entering the room, Lord Gan did not rise. Gui Jun bowed and explained himself. Lord Gan asked, “Why have you come?” Gui Jun replied, “I suffer from this grave illness and seek healing from a divine being.” Lord Gan said, “With so many attendants, I thought you came to rob the Dao. If you truly wish to be cured, send away your retinue and stay to tend the horses—then you may be healed.” Gui Jun dismissed his officials and remained to care for the horses. After three years, he still received no treatment, unaware that his illness had already been cured.

It is also said: Lord Mao studied the Dao in Qi and later attained it, residing on Mount Mao. When people with illnesses came to seek his help, he would ask them to boil ten eggs and place them inside his curtain. After a moment, Lord Mao would toss each egg back to them. If, upon breaking them, the yolks were missing, the person would recover. If the yolks contained soil, the illness would persist. This became his customary method, and the eggs remained unbroken as before.

Scroll Ten records: Feng Junda was a native of Longxi. Whenever he heard of someone, whether known to him or not, on the verge of death from illness, he would treat them with medicine, and they would recover at his touch.

It is also said: Zhao Qu, styled Zirong, was a native of Shangdang. He contracted leprosy and was near death. Someone told his family, “You must abandon him while he is still alive. If he dies at home, this disease will afflict your descendants for generations.” Thus, his family prepared a year’s worth of provisions and sent him to a stone chamber deep in the mountains. Fearing wild beasts, they barricaded the entrance with firewood. Qu, grieving and resentful, wept day and night. After over a hundred days, he suddenly saw three figures before the stone chamber and asked who they were. Qu replied, “I am a destitute man.” Realizing that no one would venture into such a remote mountain forest, he knew they must be divine beings. He knelt and pleaded, recounting his plight. The divine beings entered through the firewood as if passing through mist, untouched by any obstruction. They asked, “If you truly wish to be cured, are you willing to take medicine?”

Qu replied, “I am unworthy and full of wickedness. With this vile affliction, I have already been cast aside, death but a dawn or dusk away. If severing my feet or slicing off my nose could restore my life, I would gladly endure it—how much more so with mere medicine! I only beg the divine beings to grant me survival.”

The divine beings then bestowed upon him five *dou* each of pine seeds and pine resin. They instructed, “Consuming this will not only cure your illness but enable you to transcend the world. When you finish this, your disease will be healed—yet do not cease after recovery.”

Qu had not yet finished the medicine when his leprosy vanished entirely, his body growing robust and strong. He returned home, but his family took him for a ghost. Only after he recounted all that had transpired did they rejoice. He continued the regimen for two years, his complexion growing youthful, his skin luminous, his speed rivaling that of a flying bird.

It is further said: Elder Chen has dwelled on Zhu Yu Peak for six hundred and sixty years. The people of Zhu Yu Peak built him a hut and offer sacrifices in each of the four seasons. Yet the Elder consumes neither food nor drink, nor engages in any cultivation. For all matters, those who serve him—when afflicted by illness—beg for sacrificial water from the Elder in a vessel, and upon drinking it, are cured. Those fated to die, however, the Elder does not grant water.

*Note: Zhu Yu (纻藇) is pronounced "yú."*

The *Scripture of Great Peace*, Scroll Thirty-Three, states:
A Perfected One asked, “Why do ordinary people suffer frequent illnesses?”

The Perfected One answered: “When the spirit of the liver departs and does not return in due time, the eyes lose their brightness. When the spirit of the heart departs, the lips turn pale. When the spirit of the lungs departs, the nose becomes blocked. When the spirit of the kidneys departs, the ears grow deaf. When the spirit of the spleen departs, the mouth cannot taste sweetness. When the spirit of the head departs, one becomes dizzy and disoriented. When the spirit of the abdomen departs, the center of the belly falls into great disorder, and nothing can be transformed. When the spirits of the four limbs depart, one cannot move on one’s own. The nature of the spirit and essence is to dwell in quiet and secluded places, not in defiled and turbid ones. If one wishes to recall the spirits, one must fast and maintain purity, hang their images in a fragrant chamber, and all illnesses will vanish. Without fasting and purity, the spirits will not return to the person, and all will ascend to heaven to accuse them. Thus, people’s illnesses accumulate, and the dead do not cease.”

*Scroll Forty-Five of the Scripture of Great Peace* further states: “Heaven does not resent people having dwellings, but resents them digging too deeply into the earth, causing wounds. Some obtain the earth’s bones, some its blood. Springs are the earth’s blood, and stones are its bones. The earth is humanity’s mother; recklessly piercing one’s mother causes her to suffer grief and pain, so people are often sick and short-lived. When digging into the earth, one should not go deeper than three feet. The first foot belongs to the yang qi, which is of heaven. The second foot belongs to the qi of living things, which is of harmony. The third foot reaches the earth’s own qi, which is of yin. Beyond this lies injury to the earth’s form, and all is inauspicious. In ancient times, people relied on mountain valleys and caves, not erecting beams and pillars, so they fell ill less often. Later generations excessively harmed the earth, so they suffer more illnesses.”

It is further asked: “Nowadays, some live near flowing water and do not dig wells, yet they still fall ill and die young. Why is this?” The answer: “This is because heaven and earth are already angered by their northern neighbors, who bear the burden of one another’s faults, like a family where one member’s transgression affects their siblings. Thus, digging into the earth to obtain water—water being the earth’s blood vessels—how could one not fall ill?”

It is further asked: “Some people do not disturb the earth, yet when they build a dwelling, misfortune befalls them at once. Why is this?” The answer: “Such people act at inauspicious times, offending the spirits and inviting misfortune.” Asked, “Which spirits do they offend?” The answer: “Spirits are not one but many; one need not seek their names.”

It is further asked: “Some dig into the earth yet encounter good fortune and no illness. Why is this?” The answer: “There is always harm, but if one obtains good earth, the harm does not manifest immediately. Over time, it will still afflict people. If one obtains bad earth, illness strikes at once. Thus, great earthworks bring great misfortune, and small ones bring minor misfortune—this is the earth’s wrath, causing spirits to inflict these disasters and make people ill.”

It is further asked: “Now, when building dwellings in low-lying fields near rivers and seas, where no pillars or beams are needed, and water is found before digging three feet deep, what should be done?” The answer: “This is equally harmful. One should dig even shallower, for this is the earth’s thin skin, near its meridians. Just as a person with thick skin makes it hard to draw blood, which is still an injury, so too with thin skin: blood comes easily, but it is still an injury. The harm lies not in whether the skin is thick or thin, but in seeing blood, which is a transgression. People do not wish to be injured, so why would they wish to injure the earth and provoke its anger? Heaven and earth are humanity’s true origin, the father and mother of yin and yang. How can a child rightfully injure their parents?”

It is further asked: “Now, children receive sustenance from their mother, so people dig wells to drink. What fault is there in this?” The answer: “It is not so. People drink from their mother at the place where springs emerge. A mother’s milk is a person’s spring; just as the earth has water springs, one may drink from them. But how can one recklessly pierce their mother’s skin and drink her blood and fluids?”

*The Upper Scroll of the Perfect Writs* states: “For those gravely ill, the rescue rite is called the Rite of Righteous Fasting. For three days and nights, a person of high virtue serves as the fasting master, with five as attendants. Sincerity and joy are essential; those who have received the registers may also participate. Without joy and moral progress, one may not join the fast. Further, to rescue and release parents, teachers, lords, or fellow Daoists from great disasters and calamities, the fasting officials perform the Great Expiation Rite at an open-air altar, scattering their hair, smearing their foreheads with mud, and paying homage to the Thirty-Two Heavens. During the fast, they recite the *Zi-Wu Chapters* at midnight and noon. If their suffering is sincere, they will surely be moved. This follows the method of the *Fasting Rite of Liquid and Ashes*. Within the fasting hall, they light seven ancestral lamps to extend blessings, and five more lamps—one each at the well, stove, gate, and pavilion—to bring wisdom and fortune. Further, when the Dharma Master expounds the teachings, the assembled officials perform the rites with utmost sincerity, adhering to the proper order. If they do not fail the sick, the recipients will gradually recover. After three days of fasting, if there is evident efficacy, the master is granted thirty years of lifespan, and the attendants twenty, calculated by doubling the days of the fast.”

*Scroll One of the Pearl Satchel of the Three Caverns* concludes.
*Scroll Two of the Pearl Satchel of the Three Caverns* begins.

**On Frugality and Austerity**
*From the "Biographies of Daoist Scholars, Scroll Four" in the* Pearl Satchel of the Three Caverns, *compiled by the Tang dynasty’s Master of the Vast Sea of Dao, Wang Xuanhe*

It is said: Fan Chai, a native of Langzhong in Baxi (or written as *Chai*), possessed an elegant and refined demeanor. He never spoke of omens or portents, remaining serene and desireless, silent throughout the day. He wove tattered cloth into a net with rope, wearing coarse garments just sufficient to cover his body. Winter or summer, he went barefoot, yet his skin never cracked. He slept little in all seasons, his flesh as smooth as a maiden’s, his countenance fair. When engaging others, he always smiled, and all who knew him respected and found peace in his presence.

In the fourteenth year of the Taiyuan reign of the Liu Song dynasty, he arrived in Jingzhou and dwelled at the tail of Fucheng Isle in Zhijiang County, Nanjun, amidst the reeds. Though he had a hut, it offered no shelter from wind or frost. His bed was made of mulberry branches, stuffed with straw, and beside it stood an empty bamboo basket and a clay jar. He carried these on a pole, resting his head upon them to sleep. Sometimes he would beg for food for ten days at a time, never tasting fine grains, wine, or delicacies—only coarse millet, vegetables, and broth. A single *sheng* of food would sustain him for over ten days. He walked swiftly and spoke rapidly; unless one listened with utmost attention, his words were nearly unintelligible. He often sat in quiet reverence, his demeanor unchanged whether before high or low. He never spoke to worldly nobles or honored men, kept his eyes closed to avoid hearing music, and often lay murmuring to himself. When asked, he would say, "King Jianwu has come." Moments later, he would resume, answering other questions in ways that defied comprehension.

It is also said: He owned not a single possession, his basket and jar always empty. From afar, his patched and multicolored robes, tattered hat, and decades-old garments appeared unclean, yet upon closer inspection, they were strangely fragrant and immaculate.

*Scroll Five* further records: Liu Ningzhi, styled Zhi’an and nicknamed Changnian, was a native of Zhijiang in Nanjun. He refused all gifts from nobles and officials, dividing his family’s wealth and property among his younger brothers and nephews. Emerging from the wilderness, he built a humble abode with his own hands, taking nothing he had not earned. He and his wife lived in shared austerity, finding contentment in hardship. Unconcerned with trivial matters, they would push a cart of beans into the marketplace, selling at whatever price the day offered. Whatever food remained after their needs, they gave to the poor. Even in famine, when they themselves went without grain, they never abandoned their principles.

His wife was the daughter of Guo Quan, the Prefect of Liangzhou, who had sent her off with lavish dowry. Ningzhi distributed it all among their relatives, giving until nothing remained, and his wife bore no resentment.

It is also said: Fond of mountains and rivers, he took his wife and children to cross cliffs and ridges, building a small thatched hut. His family followed, and none knew where they ultimately settled.

*Scroll Ten* records: Zong Chao of Dongxiang, styled Yilun, was a native of Qianzou in Gaomi. When the Prince of Luling of the Liang dynasty built him halls and corridors, all were grand and resplendent, with ritual implements of exquisite craftsmanship. Yet the master’s own dwelling remained a thatched hut, unchanged. His clothing, bedding, and curtains were all simple and unadorned. He often sat upon a round, withered stump, its surface worn smooth by his knees. His straw hat and woven mat were threadbare and broken, yet he never replaced them.

It is also said: Yan Jizhi, styled Jingchu, was a native of Jurong in Danyang. He left his family to follow the Dao, eating only vegetables and wearing plain cloth, his body never covered by even an inch of silk.

*Scroll Eleven* records: Zhang Ze, styled Shi’he, was a native of Wu in Wu Commandery. Devoted to the Dao and its teachings, he drifted through the world, speaking or remaining silent as circumstances demanded. Though poor, he lived within his means, never troubled by his condition.

*Scroll Thirteen* records: Yu Chengxian, styled Chongguang, was a native of Yanling in Yingchuan. Well-versed in the *Laozi* and *Zhuangzi*, he lived with purity and frugality, wearing plain cloth and a woven mat. For over forty years, he maintained a vegetarian fast, devoting himself to teaching and expounding the Dao.

It is also said: Meng Daoyang, styled Xiaoyuan and also known as Yuan, was a native of Pingchang. Naturally kind and benevolent, he clothed himself in plain garments and ate only vegetables, owning nothing beyond the essentials. Diligent in his practice, he never grew weary, performing rituals and prostrations regardless of cold or heat.

*Scroll Fifteen* records: Dai Sheng, styled Shirong, was a native of Qu’e in Jinling. He left home in his youth and, though impoverished, once worked under Liu Biao, a local official fond of hunting. Sheng abandoned this life to enter Maoshan.

*Scroll Sixteen* records: Kong Lingchan, styled Lingchan, was a native of Shanyin in Kuaiji. After his mother’s death, he renounced wine, meat, and delicacies, living on gruel, vegetables, and plain cloth for the rest of his life.

*Scroll Eighteen* records: Deng Yuzhi, styled Xuanda, was a native of Xinye in Nanyang. He withdrew to Mount Nanyue, traveling alone and dwelling in no fixed abode. In cold and heat, he wore only tattered robes, never tying his hair after mourning. When his hair grew long, he cut it. He lived during the Yongming era.

The *Biographies of Immortals, Scroll One* states: You Bozi wore only a single garment in winter and a coarse coat in summer. His appearance changed year by year, and after decades, he appeared younger. No one could fathom his age.

The *Biographies of Divine Immortals, Scroll Four* states: Kong Yuan of Xuchang subsisted on *fuling* and pine nuts, wearing coarse clothes and eating simple fare.

Jiao Xian, styled Xiaoran, was a native of Taiyang in Hedong. At 170 years old, he ate white stones and built a thatched hut by the mountainside. His clothes were tattered, so he sold firewood in the market to buy old garments. In winter, he wore only a single layer, his complexion ruddy, resting as if in the heat of summer after drinking wine.

**On Concealing Radiance**
From the *Lower Scripture of the Great Cavern*:
The Supreme Lord of the Great Dao, his form submerged in the Eight Mysteries, his radiance concealed within the Ten Thousand Sources. Vast and boundless, he pervades the unseen, his branches marked by rosy clouds. His divine splendor stirs outwardly, his *qi* suffuses the flaming isles. In harmony with the great transformation, he dwells in the primordial void. The vast emptiness embraces the strange, and form dissolves with the transient.

*Biographies of Daoist Scholars, Scroll Five* records: Dongfang Shuo, styled Manqian, was a native of Yanci in Pingyuan.

Purity in the Dao yet concealment in its traces, clarity in essence yet obscurity in its manifestations. As for those of far-reaching mind and expansive capacity, of vast talent and brilliant eloquence, of untrammeled erudition and mastery across all fields—such was his nature.

There was also Yan Junping, whose given name was Zun, a native of Shu Commandery. He would wear no garment not befitting the Way, consume no food not in accord with it. He often practiced divination in the market of Chengdu, earning a hundred coins daily—enough for his sustenance—whereupon he would close his shop, lower the curtain, and cultivate the Dao. Profound and unwavering, he never made frivolous displays nor pursued superficial virtue. Though long secluded, he never altered his resolve, never served in office, and passed away at home in his nineties.

**Scroll Six** records: Zhu Huikai, styled Zhiyuan, was a native of Wucheng in Wu Commandery. By nature, he dwelt in seclusion, ever practicing tranquility and inner refinement. He often cut off all travel and social visits. When asked how he could remain so reclusive, he replied: *"To speak of activity is to find even the Six Directions insufficient; to speak of stillness is to find even a single room more than enough."*

**Scroll Eight** records: Tao Hongjing, styled Tongming, was a native of Pingyang in Wei Commandery. During the Ming Emperor’s reign, he retired to Mount Zhong, where he built a hermitage and concealed himself in deep seclusion.

**Scroll Thirteen** records: Yu Chengxian, styled Chongguang, was a native of Yingchuan. Twice summoned to serve as Registrar of his home province, he refused. When envoys were sent to formally appoint him, he shut his door and withdrew into seclusion, preventing them from advancing.

**Summoning Daoist Adepts**
From *Biographies of Daoist Scholars, Scroll Seven*:
Lu Xiujing, styled Yuande, was a native of Dongqian in Wu Commandery. He secluded himself on Mount Lu’s Waterfall Mountain to cultivate the Dao. Emperor Ming of the Liu-Song, desiring to propagate Daoist teachings, widely sought those of eminent virtue. Admiring the master’s character, he dispatched envoys to summon him. In the third year of the Taishi era (467 CE), the emperor issued an edict to Wang Jingzong, Governor of Jiangzhou, ordering him to urge the master’s journey to the capital with all due courtesy. The master declined on grounds of illness, yet the edicts came repeatedly. Though the emperor could not secure his presence, his admiration only deepened. Imperial messengers followed one another in an unbroken stream, for his arrival was deemed inevitable.

The Master then declared: "His Majesty is wise and far-seeing, yet deigns to regard one as unworthy as I with favor. Gazing upward, I feel the vastness of spring’s generosity; looking downward, I am deeply ashamed and fearful. Even Laozi relinquished his post as royal archivist to aid the Zhou court, and the Perfected Immortal [Zhang Daoling] set aside his golden seal and bamboo tablet to assist the Wu dynasty. Those who have attained the Dao and become lofty perfected ones still humble themselves—what am I, that I alone should preserve my own integrity?" Thereupon, he commanded his disciple Chen Piaozhi to journey to the capital.

Upon first arriving in Jiujiang, the Prince of Jiujiang inquired of him regarding the similarities and differences between Buddhism and Daoism. The Master replied: "In Buddhism, it is the *Liushen* [‘Retained in Qin’]; in Daoism, it is the Jade Emperor—these are but different paths leading to the same end." The prince and his ministers praised his words.

Upon reaching the capital, the emperor ordered the Director of the Imperial Secretariat, Ji Lin Zixuan, to convey his decree, bidding the Master reside in the Rear Hall. The Master found no joy in this and temporarily stayed at the *Biaoqi Hanghu Kezi* Abode. Imperial messengers bearing inquiries came in an unbroken stream, and both court and common folk rejoiced in anticipation.

Thereafter, the emperor commanded the Minister over the Masses, the Prince of Jian’an, and the President of the Imperial Secretariat, Yuan Can, to prepare a grand banquet of wide embrace, setting forth a seat to welcome worthies. Eminent scholars of the age were broadly assembled, and the finest minds of the time gathered at the solemn Buddhist monastery. At that time, masters of profound discourse let their eloquence flow like rivers, and learned śramaṇas advanced their arguments with sharp brilliance. Daoists and Buddhists vied in debate, each posing challenges to the other. The Master, however, set forth principles with concise words, resolving entanglements and blunting sharpness. The prince and his ministers sighed in admiration, and all present, near and far, were pleased and convinced.

When the gathering concluded, a memorial was presented to the emperor. Within ten days, another assembly was convened at the *Hualin Yanxian* Pavilion. The emperor personally attended, and all the princes and ministers gathered. The Master, wearing a deer-hide cap, paid his respects to the emperor and ascended. The Son of Heaven grew even more reverent, personally inquiring of the Dao and seeking the ultimate principle. The Master expounded upon the mysteries of the Daoist gate, elucidating its traditions and lineages, all of which reached the subtlest and most profound, none failing to embody wondrous standards. The emperor’s heart was delighted.

The prince and his ministers then asked: "We have never heard Daoists speak of the two ages [past and future]." The Master replied: "The scriptures say, ‘I know not whose child it is, but it resembles the ancestor of the Emperor.’ Since there is an ancestor, there is clearly a descendant. Since there is an ancestor and a descendant, there is clearly a middle. Zhuangzi said, ‘As it is born, so it dies’—this also clarifies the three ages. Yet the words are few and the principle profound; the world has not yet awakened to it."

The court sought to entice him with honors, but the Master remained distant and unmoved.

The Song emperor then built the Hall of Lofty Emptiness north of the capital to honor him, constructing grand edifices and widely inviting eminent companions. There, the Master broadly opened the gates of the Dao, deeply expounding its arcane classics. Court and commoner alike turned their attention to him, and both Daoists and layfolk gave him their hearts. The flourishing of Daoist teachings reached its zenith in this era.

Volume Eight states: Tao Hongjing, styled Tongming, was a native of Pingyang in Wei Commandery. Devoted to the Yellow Emperor and Laozi, he thoroughly studied the methods of the Dao and styled himself the Recluse of Huayang. Initially, he rested at Mount Gouqu in Jurong County, ascending its cliffs to seek stillness. When Emperor Ming of Qi ascended the throne, fearing discord with the hidden spirits, he urgently requested the Recluse to visit the renowned sacred peaks, offering prayers and paying respects. Thus, Tao traveled through five commanderies over three years. Upon completing his tasks, he was welcomed back and allowed to rest at Mount Zhongling. Messengers came and went several times a month, bearing lavish gifts and provisions, fulfilling every request. The court and common folk alike inundated him with letters, their frequency becoming burdensome.

Volume Nine states: In the second year of Jianwu under the Qi, Tao received an imperial edict to pray at the eastern sacred mountains and exhaustively seek the scriptures. It also states: Jiang Fuchu, a native of Yixing, came and went from Mount Mao, aspiring to dwell there. Emperor Gao of Qi built an abbey on the mountain, and in the second year of Jianyuan, he issued an edict inviting Jiang to perform Daoist rites at Chongyang Abbey.

Volume Twelve states: Meng Zhizhou, a man of Emperor Wu of Liang’s time, was broadly learned. Prince Jinghui of Liang governed Shenji and invited Zhizhou to lecture at Guangzhai Temple. When the monk Fayun arrived, he initiated the discourse. Fayun’s profound understanding was unmatched, and he treated Zhizhou with disdain. Yet as they exchanged words, Zhizhou’s expositions shone brilliantly, and the assembly of monks marveled and submitted to him.

Volume Fourteen states: Sun Youyue, styled Xuanda, was a native of Yongkang in Dongyang. He abstained from grains, consuming hemp fibers, pine, and wood. In the third year of Yongming under the Qi, he was summoned by edict to serve as abbot of Xingshi Abbey.

Volume Sixteen states: Kong Lingchan, a native of Shanyin in Kuaiji, deeply studied the essentials of the Dao and perused the entire corpus of immortals’ texts. Emperor Ming of Song established Huaixian Abbey beside the Yu Cavern and decreed that Kong reside there. He was later promoted to Grand Master for Splendid Happiness, granted the title Gentleman of the Palace, and bestowed with a deer-fur cap, a coarse fur robe, and bamboo and silk vessels. The emperor’s handwritten edict read: “You possess the bearing of the ancients. I grant you the attire of a recluse. On the day of your ascent, you may come to visit me.”

Volume Nineteen states: Xu Shizi, styled Dewei, was a native of Donghai. When Emperor Wu of Chen established Zongxu Abbey, he appointed Shizi as its abbot. Six or seven years later, Shizi passed away at the abbey. Emperor Wen issued an edict reproaching the lack of funerary provisions, and thereafter, all matters concerning his burial were supplied by the imperial court.

**Ritual of Presenting Tablets to Mountains and Waters**
*The Fourteenth Scripture of the Cavernous Spirit* states: Those who study long life and preserve the luminous spirits must rely on the benevolence of mountains and the wisdom of waters, for these are the foundations of movement and stillness. To rely on benevolence is to be still and long-lived; to rely on wisdom is to be active and joyful. Joy is near water, longevity like mountains. Dwelling in mountains and delighting in waters is the method of long life. One should present tablets bearing one’s name and pay homage to the spirits of mountains and waters. The numinous sovereigns of the emperors protect the virtuous, enabling them to cultivate benevolence and wisdom and attain long life as divine immortals.

On the eight seasonal nodes at the hours of *yin* and *wu*, write in cinnabar on a white hibiscus tablet: “Your humble descendant, [name], of [state, commandery, county, village, and rank], seeks long life, transfers his register to the Great Clarity, alters the record of death, and inscribes the talisman of life.” When the tablet is filled, face the mountain’s king, burn incense, open the censer, bow twice, read the tablet, bow twice again, and kneel. Wrap the tablet in blue paper, bind it with blue silk, bow twice more, and place it upon a rock. Let no one witness this. Close the censer and depart without looking back.

For waters, board a boat and drift to the center of the current. Bow twice, then face the water’s king, open the censer, bow from the heart, read the tablet, bow again, wrap and bind it as before, and tie it to a clean stone to sink it. Beginning at the start of spring, perform this twenty-four times over three years, and you will surely summon the perfected. If, due to public or private matters, you miss a single node, begin anew. If the count is complete but no response comes, your merit is slight and your conduct coarse—redouble your efforts and cultivate with precision. Over time, spirits will descend, and you will know good and ill fortune, understanding both the hidden and manifest. Cease only when the Dao is achieved.

*The Upper Jade Instructions of the Red Writ* states: The Primordial Numinous Treasure addressed the Water Sovereign, cutting away the tablet of sins. The superior method is as follows: “The Yellow Emperor of the Numinous Treasure, [your name], aged [years], born on [month, day], yearns for divine transcendence and long life without death, with the Three Origins preserved together. O Nine Prefectures’ Water Sovereign, Twelve River Sources, and the great spirits of the Jiang, Hai, Huai, and Ji rivers and the vast seas, I beseech you to erase my sinful name, report it to the Nine Heavens, and present it to the Water Palace. By golden dragon courier, [your name] submits this announcement to the local earth god of [state] on the first day of [month, year].”

Write this in cinnabar on a silverwood tablet, wrap it in blue paper, bind it with blue silk, and have a golden dragon bear the tablet into the depths of the Three Rivers. Initially, attach nine golden clasps to the tablet before sinking it; thereafter, this is unnecessary. After three presentations, cease. Cast the tablet into a clear, cold abyss, face north, click your teeth three times, and upon finishing the reading, pray: “Supreme and exalted Five Elder Lords of the Primordial Beginning, perfected beings of the ten directions, divine immortals of Mount Taihua, the Red Writ’s proclamation opens all that is hidden. Ascending to the Nine Heavens, I request the descent of the Jade Writ, the sun, moon, and five planets, the Northern Dipper’s seven origins. In the center, I announce this to the Five Peaks and the sacred mountains of the four directions; below, to the rivers and seas, the Twelve Water Sources, the Nine Prefectures’ Water Sovereign, and the great spirits of the vast seas. Today, I make this supreme announcement, unfolding ten thousand wishes. I present the jade tablet, beseeching the erasure of my sins, those of my thousand ancestors and ten thousand forebears, the nine clans and all kin, and the sins that have stained me. May all be universally erased, their roots severed, their names recorded in the Water Palace and proclaimed before the Emperor. May my seven generations of ancestors depart from the Eight Difficulties and ascend to the Nine Heavens, clothed and fed by nature. May my sins be released and dispersed, known to all spirits. I entreat the golden dragon to bind the Water Officials in covenant. As I have stated, let the golden dragon courier transmit this.”

It is further stated: The Primordial Numinous Treasure announces to the Five Marchmounts and the sacred mountains, seeking the erasure of sins and the attainment of transcendence.

The supreme method declares: The Numinous Treasure Yellow Emperor Master, so-and-so, born in such-and-such a year, month, and day, of such-and-such age, whose fate is bound to the Yellow Emperor and whose name is registered at Mount Tai, yearns for divine transcendence and immortality, to ascend through the world and soar into the Upper Clarity.

O Perfected Ones of the Five Marchmounts, most divine and numinous, I beseech you to erase my name from the ledgers of sin and inscribe it in the Nine Heavens. I present this petition at the sacred mountain, to be transmitted by the golden dragon courier.

On the first day of such-and-such a month and year, I, the disciple, submit this announcement at such-and-such a state and such-and-such a marchmount.

Inscribe this in vermilion on a silver-wood tablet, wrap it in blue paper, bind it with blue silk, affix nine golden clasps, and bury it with a golden dragon at the marchmount of one’s fate, all in accordance with the above method.

At the marchmount of one’s fate, face east, knock the teeth nine times, and upon finishing the reading of the tablet, recite the prayer:

Mysterious Elder, Radiant and Clear,
Primordial One who oversees the Perfected.
Five Elders of the Emperor,
Vermilion Script and Cinnabar Texts.
Heaven and Earth in their origin,
Governing the Three Primes.
Drawing in qi and summoning assembly,
Hastening the ascent of transcendents.
Supreme talismanic mandate,
A single Dao spanning the Ten Heavens.
Sun, moon, and stellar lodges,
Five Marchmounts and sacred mountains.
Above the heavens, below the earth,
Vast spirits of the deep,
Overseeing life and recording destiny,
At the Southern Three Gates.
Binding the jade tablet,
Inscribing names in the celestial register.
Erasing the books of sin,
Promoting to the ranks of imperial ministers.
A thousand ancestors and ten thousand forebears,
Nine clans and all kin,
All delivered from the Five Paths and Eight Difficulties,
Departing the Three Evils,
Their souls ascending to the Nine Heavens.
Liberated in life and death,
Preserved for ten thousand kalpas.
Today I make this supreme announcement,
Heard by all spirits.
I entreat the golden clasps
To bind the Perfected Officials in covenant.
As I have stated,
Let the golden dragon courier transmit this.

It is further stated: The Primordial Numinous Treasure announces to the Nine Earthly Soil Sovereigns, beseeching the obliteration of sins, the proclamation of names, and the attainment of transcendence.

The supreme method declares: The Numinous Treasure Yellow Emperor Master, so-and-so, born in such-and-such a year, month, and day, of such-and-such age, whose fate is bound to the Nine Heavens and whose register is held by the Central Dipper, yearns for divine transcendence and immortality, to ascend through the world and soar into the Upper Clarity.

O Central August One of the Nine Earths,
Yellow Spirits of Wu and Ji,
Five Emperors of the Soil Palace,
I beseech you to erase my record of sins
And inscribe it in the Great Mystery.
I present this petition at the Central Palace,
Submitting the tablet to record my name.
Today I pledge my sincerity,
To be transmitted by the golden dragon courier.

On the first day of such-and-such a month and year, I, the disciple, submit this announcement at the central precinct of such-and-such a commandery, state, village, and hamlet.

Inscribe this in vermilion on a silver-wood tablet, wrap it in blue paper, bind it with blue silk, affix nine golden clasps and one golden dragon, and bury it at the central palace of one’s dwelling, all in accordance with the above method.

In the central courtyard, face the sovereign, knock the teeth twelve times, and upon finishing the reading of the tablet, recite the prayer:

Heaven unfolds, Earth extends,
Nine qi divide the numinous.
Three luminaries share the same talisman,
Ten directions shine in clarity.
Five Elder Emperors,
Opening the Perfected and governing life.
Cinnabar Script and Jade Texts,
Yao’s primordial essence ascends.
Universally announced to the world,
Nine Soil Sovereigns and numinous spirits.
Today I make this supreme announcement,
All transcendents confirm my birth.
I yearn for my divine transcendence,
I present my jade name.
Great August One of the Soil Palace,
Erase my sins and punishments.
Submit the tablet to the Upper Palace,
Inscribe it in the registers of Splendor and Azure.
Ascend and soar,
Heaven and Earth share the numinous.
Nine clans and all kin,
Their decayed bones renewed in glory.
Their souls ascend to the Southern Palace,
Transformed in the transcendent courts.
On this auspicious day, I proclaim my mandate,
Heard by all spirits.
I entreat the golden clasps
To manifest the covenant of utmost sincerity.
As I have stated,
Let the golden dragon courier transmit this.

The Dao declares: To deliver the people of all heavens, liberating them from the Five Paths and Eight Difficulties, enabling them to pass beyond the Three Evils, and ensuring they encounter the Dao in every lifetime, one must present the Three Prime Jade Tablets on the days of the Eight Nodal Points and the jiazi cycle, erasing the ledgers of past sins and proclaiming names to Heaven. Each matter is to be presented three times before all heavens, the Three Officials, and the Nine Palaces, with successive transmissions and separate submissions to Heaven. All spirits will revere and protect, the Five Emperors will attend, the Three Realms will receive and welcome, gates and thresholds will be ordered, the earthly spirits of the world and the deities of sun and moon will come to pay homage. A thousand demons and ten thousand spirits dare not rashly interfere. With resolve firm and pure, all aspirations will be fulfilled.
